  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_73ae7eb2-36a8-486c-8bae-975a1ba3cf3d'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer6901233309971951055">From the winner of the Michael L. Printz Award and the Carnegie Medal, a work of astonishing intimacy and depth <br/>Using a pillow book as her form, nineteen-year-old Cordelia Kenn sets out to write out her life for her unborn daughter. What emerges is a portrait of an extraordinary girl, who writes frankly of love, sex, poetry, nature, faith, and of herself in the world. Her </span>
  <span id="freeText6901233309971951055" style="display:none">From the winner of the Michael L. Printz Award and the Carnegie Medal, a work of astonishing intimacy and depth <br />Using a pillow book as her form, nineteen-year-old Cordelia Kenn sets out to write out her life for her unborn daughter. What emerges is a portrait of an extraordinary girl, who writes frankly of love, sex, poetry, nature, faith, and of herself in the world. Her thoughts range widely: on Shakespeare and breasts, periods and piano playing, friendship and trees, consciousness and sleep, and much more besides. As she writes of William Blacklin, the boy she chooses as her first lover, or Julie, the teacher who encourages her spiritual life, Cordelia maddens, fascinates, and ultimately seduces the reader. This is a character never to be forgotten from a writer at the height of his powers.</span>

<div id="review_6209331" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6209331" />
    <a title="Shelley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/108756-shelley"><img alt="Shelley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1180665895p2/108756.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6209331?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shelley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shelley" href="/user/show/108756-shelley">Shelley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/108756-shelley?shelf=youth">youth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/108756-shelley?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6209331" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11286037330169998417">I cannot believe I actually read this book. Okay, I kind of skimmed the first few hundred pages. But I was so sucked into Cordelia's story that I read the remaining six hundred. (Mostly after midnight and in increments of 150-200 pages. Damn two week check outs.)<br/><br/>It's not a book I'd have thought I'd like. The writing style is kind of lyrical at times, and I have limited patience for writing like that. There's also the a/b page phenomenon, which was odd. (I preferred the b pages.) As other reviews</span>
  <span id="freeText11286037330169998417" style="display:none">I cannot believe I actually read this book. Okay, I kind of skimmed the first few hundred pages. But I was so sucked into Cordelia's story that I read the remaining six hundred. (Mostly after midnight and in increments of 150-200 pages. Damn two week check outs.)<br /><br />It's not a book I'd have thought I'd like. The writing style is kind of lyrical at times, and I have limited patience for writing like that. There's also the a/b page phenomenon, which was odd. (I preferred the b pages.) As other reviews said, the voice is odd for a 15-19 year old. Honestly, to me, it felt like Vicky Austin in L'Engle's books. The same sort of conversations and word choices that I've never run across in other books or in real life. I love the Austin books, although it felt odd to me there, too. But there is precedence. *g*<br /><br />When I got it, I never thought I'd read the whole thing, so I skimmed the end. All I could think was that if I'd read 768 pages only to read that first line of the last book (there are six total in here), I would be furious. So I at least knew what I was getting into, but I was still upset when it happened. <br /><br />Honestly, despite all the things about it that would normally put me off, I loved every bit of it. Cordelia was a great character, real and flawed and lovely, and I'm glad I knew her.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11286037330169998417" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_114977841" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/114977841" />
    <a title="Kristina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3735975-kristina"><img alt="Kristina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451685788p2/3735975.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/114977841?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristina" href="/user/show/3735975-kristina">Kristina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">someone who likes long books, a girl, someone seeking inspiration, someone who likes love stories</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Kristina by: </span>
      a male friend
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer114977841" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17580037330339822573">I first picked up this book when a male friend recommended it to me. I've encountered bits and pieces of it through him and when I first went to pick up the book, its size astonished me. When I began to read it, my astonishment only grew - a book with a female narrator, practically spilling herself into these books/boxes that she kept? At sixteen, I instantly liked it, and fell in love with Cordelia. This book immediately became a big source of inspiration for me; I loved the way she could jump </span>
  <span id="freeText17580037330339822573" style="display:none">I first picked up this book when a male friend recommended it to me. I've encountered bits and pieces of it through him and when I first went to pick up the book, its size astonished me. When I began to read it, my astonishment only grew - a book with a female narrator, practically spilling herself into these books/boxes that she kept? At sixteen, I instantly liked it, and fell in love with Cordelia. This book immediately became a big source of inspiration for me; I loved the way she could jump from one thing to the next, I loved her essays, lists, "mopes", I loved the lyrical tone of her writing. A couple hundred pages in I decided to look into some information about the author (I kept thinking "Aidan" could possibly be a female) and I was shocked to learn that he is a man! His portrayal of this female protagonist is exceptional. <br />The length of this book was no issue for me - it's safe to say that I finished it in just under two weeks. I couldn't put it down, and the only obstacle I had was the split a/b pages. It wasn't something that I've seen before, but I shrugged it away.<br />The ending blew me away. I always steer clear of flipping forward and spoiling the ending or plot twist of a book for myself, so when I finally reached the final book, it hit me harder than I could possibly expect. I cried impossibly hard, especially considering the fact that I had already been reading for two hours straight (I was nearing the end and desperately wanted to finish that night) and I can honestly say, no other book has drawn this many tears out of me. The success of this book lies in the fact that it draws the reader in - Cordelia is so believable, honest and flawed, yet terribly lovable, at least for me. As a young girl who slowly grows older through the hundreds of pages, it's easy for others to fall in love with her insights and passions. After reading for a long stretch of time and diving into the very essence of Cordelia, the loss of this character simply breaks your heart. <br />All in all, this book got my creative ball rolling and at times I would catch myself thinking back to it. I recommended it to a few friends, but most of them never bothered to finish, to my surprise. I am planning to re-read it in the near future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17580037330339822573" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9901304?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gizmology" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gizmology" href="/user/show/525476-gizmology">Gizmology</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">teens and twentysomethings or nostalgic reminiscing adults </span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9901304" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3932217439031848557">I was loving this book -- the first section about her developing relationship with the boy she's decided to lose her virginity to was great -- all the anxiety and excitement and fear of it rang really true to me. BUT then there's THAT PART that everyone mentions, where there are suddenly two parallel storylines, A and B, for more than a hundred pages, like page 191-A, page 192-B, 193-A, 194-B, etc.etc., so you have to decide whether to go back and forth or read all of A and THEN all of B... AAAA</span>
  <span id="freeText3932217439031848557" style="display:none">I was loving this book -- the first section about her developing relationship with the boy she's decided to lose her virginity to was great -- all the anxiety and excitement and fear of it rang really true to me. BUT then there's THAT PART that everyone mentions, where there are suddenly two parallel storylines, A and B, for more than a hundred pages, like page 191-A, page 192-B, 193-A, 194-B, etc.etc., so you have to decide whether to go back and forth or read all of A and THEN all of B... AAAAAARGH. I thought I should read them simultaneously -- A, B, A, B, because the point seemed to be that they were side-by-side narratives, but it is NOT convenient to keep stopping at the end of a page to switch to the other story. I kept forgetting, or just not wanting to stop, and just reading three A's and having to go back and catch up with the B's, or vice-versa. <br /><br />I guess I *can* admire the intent of this conceit, and maybe I can see that things like this might be a deliberately anti-convenience protest (I was going to say anti-Starbucks, but I'm actually sitting in a Starbucks that I love right now, whoops, so maybe that's the whole problem), but when I read fiction, I want to be immersed. Completely. Totally. Immersed. I don't want to be reminded every thirty seconds that I'm just reading made-up pages by having to take myself outside the story and flip the pages backwards. As I'm a confirmed member of the Escapist School of fiction reading, *flow* is absolutely crucial to my reading experience. So do I need an attitude adjustment? Maybe. I'll go back and try to keep reading and see if I can get through the A-B's by reminding myself that this is an existential challenge, not a New Millenium McHappyRead. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3932217439031848557" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2761569" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2761569" />
    <a title="Cara Marie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/162961-cara-marie"><img alt="Cara Marie" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2761569?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cara Marie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cara Marie" href="/user/show/162961-cara-marie">Cara Marie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099417767" href="/book/show/479470.This_is_All">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2761569" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15052043904183664314">I was thinking about this again as I'm reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18185.The_Pillow_Book' title='The Pillow Book by Sei Shōnagon' rel='nofollow noopener'> The Pillow Book</a> that serves partly as its inspiration. I liked the way it used a variety of writings to tell the story - not just the protagonist Cordelia's diary, but her essays, musings and 'mopes' - the latter being poetry of debatable quality. It was a lovely book. Cordelia could be annoying, and sometimes seemed too sort of well-adjusted liberal in her attitudes towards sex and her body to be real, but it's hard to tell if that was unrealistic</span>
  <span id="freeText15052043904183664314" style="display:none">I was thinking about this again as I'm reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18185.The_Pillow_Book" title="The Pillow Book by Sei Shōnagon" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Pillow Book</a> that serves partly as its inspiration. I liked the way it used a variety of writings to tell the story - not just the protagonist Cordelia's diary, but her essays, musings and 'mopes' - the latter being poetry of debatable quality. It was a lovely book. Cordelia could be annoying, and sometimes seemed too sort of well-adjusted liberal in her attitudes towards sex and her body to be real, but it's hard to tell if that was unrealistic, or just part of the way she could really be quite infuriating. For the most part, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/58103.Aidan_Chambers" title="Aidan Chambers" rel="nofollow noopener"> Aidan Chambers</a> wrote a convincing young woman - at first I wondered if I was just confused and Aidan could in fact be a woman's name. Nope. Man in his seventies. Quite amazing.<br /><br />I should probably read his other books, because I think he must really be an incredible writer. It's a book that questions itself - it's a novel but it's not really a novel. Not just the mix of Cordelia's writings, but in one section, two things run simulationeously - the telling of Cordelia's story, and the things she's writing at the time, essays and poetry critiques and all sorts, each printed every second page. I went through the right pages first, and then went back and read the left pages. It was intense, but it was for a purpose. <br /><br />And I like that he's not afraid to be literary, or to have a character who thinks so deeply. She's not the most likable of characters, but she knows herself, she's got guts, and you can tell the author respects her even when she's at her most typically teenage. I hope I can create something so awesome when I'm seventy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15052043904183664314" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_59654507" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/59654507" />
    <a title="Meredith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1212245-meredith"><img alt="Meredith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438123878p2/1212245.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/59654507?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meredith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meredith" href="/user/show/1212245-meredith">Meredith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780810995505" href="/book/show/3357706-this-is-all">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1212245-meredith?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1212245-meredith?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1212245-meredith?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1212245-meredith?shelf=england">england</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer59654507&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer59654507" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14960591462642398964">(I have marked this as spoilerish in content, but wish to reiterate here that this review contains MAJOR spoilers.)<br/><br/>If I could, I would give this book 3.5 stars; however, since that isn't an option, I will give it 4. I try very hard not to grade a book down when the ending doesn't go my way. Some of the best books I have read have had disappointing/ambiguous/depressing endings, but ones generally in keeping with the tenor of the narrative. But I do not enjoy cheap narrative tricks (i.e., contrive</span>
  <span id="freeText14960591462642398964" style="display:none">(I have marked this as spoilerish in content, but wish to reiterate here that this review contains MAJOR spoilers.)<br /><br />If I could, I would give this book 3.5 stars; however, since that isn't an option, I will give it 4. I try very hard not to grade a book down when the ending doesn't go my way. Some of the best books I have read have had disappointing/ambiguous/depressing endings, but ones generally in keeping with the tenor of the narrative. But I do not enjoy cheap narrative tricks (i.e., contrived "twists", Hollywood tearjerker sleights of pen, or saccharine big-red-bow endings). <br /><br />Unfortunately, Book Five of this narrative involved a rather unrealistic big-red-bow sequence of serendipities; jobs, income, dwellings, office help, and even rent waivers fell from the sky and into Will's and Cordelia's laps. After so much struggle, their starting a life together felt somewhat effortless. These were college-age kids trying to strike out on their own. Where was the ramen, so to speak? Where was the initial financial struggle that couples even a decade older face? Simply put, where was the realism? Perhaps Chambers knew that he was running low on real estate, that his epic adolescent tome was already several inches thick and in need of an ending, so he had Cordelia apologize on his behalf for rushing through this part of the narrative. Unfortunately, in doing so, he forfeited some of the overarching realism that made this story so endearing. <br /><br />Book Six, however, was when this book jumped the Heinian shark for me, and an otherwise unique narrative inspired by a timeless Japanese classic became a hackneyed tearjerker. Let me preface this by remarking that this story was a bold endeavor from the get-go--a male author taking on the psyche of a teenage girl. Chambers did the feminine adolescent mind justice throughout much of this book, with a few minor missteps here and there. However, Book Six was where he made his most egregious miscalculation. In killing off Cordelia and finishing the story from Will's point of view, he essentially broke the promise of the first five books--that this was Cordelia's story, her psyche, her gift to her child. She was not allowed to finish her own story; instead, she was disposed of (literally) at her beloved Uffington White Horse and we were left with nothing but Will's closing statements. We experienced some of the most pivotal moments of her life--her wedding, her baby's naming, and her few months of motherhood before death--through her husband's eyes. Thus, both narrator and reader were robbed of something vital. <br /><br />Cordelia's voice carried us through nearly 800 pages of teenage angst, heartache, joy, musings, and relationships. It strikes me as strange that, in the final pages, the author took away her voice and replaced it with that of a man. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14960591462642398964" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_54362819" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/54362819" />
    <a title="Hallie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1280214-hallie"><img alt="Hallie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562279936p2/1280214.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/54362819?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hallie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hallie" href="/user/show/1280214-hallie">Hallie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Kelley</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer54362819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4861699538679904596">I hesitated between three and four stars for this book. On the one hand, it had some excellent qualities: sheer length (~800 pages) without becoming dull, for the most part well-developed characters, a non-linear structure that is intriguing and ends up mostly making sense, and the fact that a middle-aged man successfully and convincingly pulled off a first-person narrative in the character of a teenage girl. On the other hand, while not as creepily all-consuming as Twilight, this novel's love s</span>
  <span id="freeText4861699538679904596" style="display:none">I hesitated between three and four stars for this book. On the one hand, it had some excellent qualities: sheer length (~800 pages) without becoming dull, for the most part well-developed characters, a non-linear structure that is intriguing and ends up mostly making sense, and the fact that a middle-aged man successfully and convincingly pulled off a first-person narrative in the character of a teenage girl. On the other hand, while not as creepily all-consuming as Twilight, this novel's love story is of the passionate "love of my life discovered at age 15" variety that I find a) hard to buy into and b) faintly nauseating. So while I was convinced I was living in the mind of a late teenage girl, I didn't always like being there. On the other hand, when she was talking about anything other than being in love, I felt quite at home with the character, and the narration is full of vaguely philosophical digressions, amusing monologues and literary references in addition to the actual plot (really, I'd say they were more interesting than the actual plot). It reminded me strongly of a lighter but better-executed version of Special Topics in Calamity Physics. In sum: four stars for the idea, two for the plot, three for overall realization.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4861699538679904596" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_610076789" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/610076789" />
    <a title="Inger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20151580-inger"><img alt="Inger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455398836p2/20151580.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/610076789?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inger" href="/user/show/20151580-inger">Inger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780810995505" href="/book/show/3357706-this-is-all">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20151580-inger?shelf=finished-2013">finished-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer610076789" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15948180565677833694">I can't believe I finally finished this book. I have never had this much problem with finishing a book before. And I've got to say, This Is All is one of the most unique books I have ever read. <br/><br/>The book is... strange. I have never had so many mixed feeling reading a book before. My feelings have been ranging between love, hate, admiration, fascination, disgust, anger, confusion, enchantment and frustration. I wanted to throw the book across the room frequently. It took me around TWO YEARS to fin</span>
  <span id="freeText15948180565677833694" style="display:none">I can't believe I finally finished this book. I have never had this much problem with finishing a book before. And I've got to say, This Is All is one of the most unique books I have ever read. <br /><br />The book is... strange. I have never had so many mixed feeling reading a book before. My feelings have been ranging between love, hate, admiration, fascination, disgust, anger, confusion, enchantment and frustration. I wanted to throw the book across the room frequently. It took me around TWO YEARS to finish the book because at times it infuriated me so much I had to put it away and not pick it up again for more than six times. I only finished it after giving it a third chance. <br /><br />Oh, Cordelia, where to start? She's such a... teenager. I honestly could relate so much to her at times as I read this book between the ages of 15 and 18 (my birthday is in December, therefore two years). The way Chambers describes how the teenage mind works astonishes me, how can a man of that age understand the thoughts of a teenager so clearly and convey them so vividly and realistically? At the same time, there were a lot of things that were a bit too much. Honestly Cordelia, when I don't want to know what you prefer a penis to be like, your daughter will certainly not want to know it. Also, I was absolutely revolted by how Cordelia tells us she want's to bury her face in Will's armpit hair and his pubes. Honestly. I've been so in love I've been out of my mind and acted like a complete idiot, and that though has NEVER crossed my mind. Chest, yes. Pubes, NO. <br /><br />The book is built up of different "pillow books" and each book is quite different. I think the part of the book that was most annoying to me is the part where a very creepy thing happens. In that part the book is divided in small chapters that both follow the story line and talk about completely different things. I felt like all I wanted to know was what was going to happen, so I had to skip a lot and go back, which honestly was quite annoying. Generally I do like Chambers' writing. The most boring part of the book to me was all the spiritual stuff, I found it slightly unnecessary and completely uninteresting. <br /><br /><br />The book was so hard for me to finish and I hated it with a passion at times, but I couldn't help to love it all the same. I found myself caressing the book when I'd put it away and after I finished it, the third time I gave it a go that is. You know, the way you'd pat your cat or the cheek of a baby. The books ending was devastating, and it made me love it more. I might be weird, but I gotta admit I love myself a sad ending from time to time. There's a reason why I can't possibly love a movie more than I love Titanic. <br /><br />All in all, it is a book that should definitely be read. If I'll read it again, I have no idea, but I do know that it was worth it. I would recommend it to anyone who hasn't read it, but I'm not saying you will feel the same things I did. Maybe you'll hate it through and through, or love it through and through. All the same, do it, experience it and feel everything it can make you feel to the fullest. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15948180565677833694" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_521128367" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/521128367" />
    <a title="Emily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16950629-emily"><img alt="Emily" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/521128367?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily" href="/user/show/16950629-emily">Emily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer521128367" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer678537586566144231">This is by far my absolute favorite Realistic Fiction book.<br/>I've read it twice now, and I can't decide which time I liked better.<br/>The first time was an interesting experience, of course, because I was able to be surprised by the plot and meet the characters. However, reading it a second time was like wrapping myself in a warm blanket with a cup of hot cocoa. Not only did I get to return to the wonderfully developed characters, but different aspects of the story stuck out to me anew that might've g</span>
  <span id="freeText678537586566144231" style="display:none">This is by far my absolute favorite Realistic Fiction book.<br />I've read it twice now, and I can't decide which time I liked better.<br />The first time was an interesting experience, of course, because I was able to be surprised by the plot and meet the characters. However, reading it a second time was like wrapping myself in a warm blanket with a cup of hot cocoa. Not only did I get to return to the wonderfully developed characters, but different aspects of the story stuck out to me anew that might've gotten lost in the folds the first time.<br />What really strikes me, is how well a male author can portray a female teenager. I found I was really able to relate to her, and where I couldn't relate I was able to find a new way of looking at things.<br />The way the book is formatted does get a little odd when the Parts A and B to one of the inner-books spring up, but my recommendation is to read it page-by-page. This way, you'll be able to 1) read it the way the author seems to want you, and 2) connect the different parts together--i.e. Cordelia's random entries with her actual life.<br />I definitely plan to read it yet again soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="678537586566144231" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_385478025" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/385478025" />
    <a title="Lily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4713523-lily"><img alt="Lily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543681021p2/4713523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/385478025?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lily" href="/user/show/4713523-lily">Lily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099417767" href="/book/show/479470.This_is_All">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4713523-lily?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4713523-lily?shelf=favourite-books">favourite-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4713523-lily?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer385478025" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9515496214535764723">If there is one book you need to read, it is this one. <br/><br/>When I first came across this, I was intrigued. It seemed so frank, so honest, that I was drawn to it. For example, take the back cover description:<br/><br/><i>"Using a pillow book as her form, nineteen-year-old Cordelia Kenn sets out to write out her life for her unborn daughter. What emerges is a portrait of an extraordinary girl, who writes frankly of love, sex, poetry, nature, faith, and of herself in the world. Her thoughts range widely: on Shakesp</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9515496214535764723" style="display:none">If there is one book you need to read, it is this one. <br /><br />When I first came across this, I was intrigued. It seemed so frank, so honest, that I was drawn to it. For example, take the back cover description:<br /><br /><i>"Using a pillow book as her form, nineteen-year-old Cordelia Kenn sets out to write out her life for her unborn daughter. What emerges is a portrait of an extraordinary girl, who writes frankly of love, sex, poetry, nature, faith, and of herself in the world. Her thoughts range widely: on Shakespeare and breasts, periods and piano playing, friendship and trees, consciousness and sleep, and much more besides. As she writes of William Blacklin, the boy she chooses as her first lover, or Julie, the teacher who encourages her spiritual life, Cordelia maddens, fascinates, and ultimately seduces the reader." </i><br /><br />Shakespeare, nature, poetry, sex. Those were the four words that stood out for me. I was fifteen, the age Cordelia herself is when the anecdotes begin. But I wouldn’t get to read <i>This Is All</i> until eighteen months or so later, when I was approaching seventeen (perhaps wise, since the content can be very adult). <br /><br />However, when I did, I was enthralled. What perhaps you should know is that this book does not have an ordinary female teenage voice. It isn’t like Sarah Dessen, or Cathy Hopkins, or Judy Blume. Cordelia Kenn describes her life in anecdotes so lyrical that it resonated completely with me, a fellow writer. There are boys mentioned, but the romance she pursues with Will Blacklin is mature and strangely out-of-time. There’s no abundance of social media involved, only email and text. School is rarely involved, save for Cordelia’s discussions with her favourite teacher. I think that’s what I like about it. The fact that it isn’t Americanised or overtly modern, as so many teenage books are, nor full of dull school descriptions and teen parties and drinking and suchlike. <br /><br /><i>(Apologies to you, America. I have nothing against you. It’s just books like The Princess Diaries or Just Listen or Looking for Alaska or Confessions of Georgia Nicholson never really resonated with me. The voices - despite one of those being British - never struck me as real or sincere.) </i><br /><br />Being someone who reads, finding a character like Cordelia was like stumbling across a goldmine. She reads voraciously, writes voraciously, and writes in such a way that makes me go, “Yes! That! I understand that completely! I feel that too!”<br /><br />The book is funny, sad, raw, and beautiful. It is divided into six sections, or ‘pillow books’. (This is based off an ancient Japanese way of journal keeping, where notes and thoughts and memories would be written down and then put into one’s pillow or pillow case. The main example is <i>The Pillow Book</i> by Sei Shōnagon, which Cordelia bases her own structure upon.) The pillow books focus on specific times in her life, from the age of fifteen to nineteen. It is an unusual and engaging structure, one I’d never come across before. Books Two and Three are written on alternating sides - which can be disconcerting to some, as you have to read one section first and then go back and read the other - but I never found it a problem. In fact, it made me enjoy the novel even more. <br /><br />It’s often said that books can change lives. After reading this beautiful novel, I wholeheartedly agree. <i>This Is All</i> utterly altered the way I look at things, and wherever I travel, the book accompanies me. Without gushing, I love it. Going back to it is like greeting an old friend, and since the novel is made up of anecdotes, I can always delve in wherever I please, and read from there. <br /><br /><i>This Is All</i> is exquisite. It is a masterpiece. I do not use that word often, but I feel it is appropriate now. If I could write a book half as wonderful as this one, I would be happy.<br /><br />I love it as much as I do Shakespeare. As much as <i>Cordelia</i> loves Shakespeare. <br /><br />And that should say a lot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9515496214535764723" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_680945812" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/680945812" />
    <a title="Melissa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11070415-melissa"><img alt="Melissa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1342638117p2/11070415.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/680945812?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa" href="/user/show/11070415-melissa">Melissa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11070415-melissa?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11070415-melissa?shelf=favorite-stories">favorite-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11070415-melissa?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11070415-melissa?shelf=set-in-the-uk">set-in-the-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11070415-melissa?shelf=teenagers">teenagers</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer680945812" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5632645551030504542">I read this book when I was a Freshman in high school. I can genuinely say it's one of the most intriguing, honest books that I've read. Cordelia Kenn propositions a boy that she's liked for some time, William Blacklin. Now, I think this is what really got me interested in the book; she is very straight forward, and that seems a rarity now a days when it comes to a female protagonist. What was her proposition? That William has sex with her before her sixteenth birthday, because according to stud</span>
  <span id="freeText5632645551030504542" style="display:none">I read this book when I was a Freshman in high school. I can genuinely say it's one of the most intriguing, honest books that I've read. Cordelia Kenn propositions a boy that she's liked for some time, William Blacklin. Now, I think this is what really got me interested in the book; she is very straight forward, and that seems a rarity now a days when it comes to a female protagonist. What was her proposition? That William has sex with her before her sixteenth birthday, because according to studies, the average teenager loses her virginity at age sixteen. Of course, Cordelia couldn't have this, because she doesn't want to be average.<br />Fast forward, and you have two awkward teenagers who don't quite know what they're doing, but seem to do quite well together despite that fact. There happen to be some...cliche moments in their relationship--which lasts until the end of the book, kind of-- A couple years later, when Cordelia visits Will in college, she becomes obsessed with the idea that Will is having an affair with one of his best friends. In Cordelia's mind, Will can't have a good female friend without there being some type of sexual element. When she becomes set on the idea that Will is being unfaithful--which may be in part because Will spends more time with his gal friend than with Cordelia. Cordelia then embarks in an affair of her own, whom happens to be her boss. And plenty more happens, which I honestly shouldn't tell you; if you're so interested, just read the fricken book yourself.<br />Now, you may be wondering, If you like the book so much, why give it only four stars? <br /> I'll tell you why!<br />Because the complete middle of the book is split literally in two: one page marked "A" and and the opposite "B." To be honest, I simple brain couldn't figure out which part to read."A" contained some of her poetry and some stories, which were quite frankly not very interesting; "B" continued on like the first section of the book. If I remember correctly, this section contains Will and Cordelia's rough break-up, which promptly thereafter the story goes into another part, structurally the same as the first "Pillow Book." <br />When the reader reaches the Epilogue, there is surprise and a cascade of tears. I think I would ruin completely the shock of it if I wrote more about it, so I shall end there.<br />All in all, I think I was most impressed on how Aidan Chambers, a male author, was able to so authentically capture the teenage female psyche. Now, I've read quite a few books about a female written by a male author, but there was something incredibly sincere about Cordelia, even when you wanted to punch her fictional face because sometimes she could just be so incredibly <i>naive</i>. <br />Despite the sometimes incredibly explicit content, this book is incredibly insightful, in the mind of a teenage girl who is really just trying to find herself. <br /><br />“There are times when you don't know yourself. There are times when you don't want to know yourself. There are times when you want to be what you have never allowed yourself to be before.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="5632645551030504542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84419709?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aileen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aileen" href="/user/show/2206485-aileen">Aileen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780810995505" href="/book/show/3357706-this-is-all">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2206485-aileen?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84419709" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6830437141643732525">I honestly don't think there will ever be a book that not only captures the essence of girls, but is also the most heart-wrenching and tear inducing book I will ever read. This book starts off when Cordelia Kenn is only 15. 808 pages later she is twenty and two months old. The reader, girl or boy, learns more about life, love, girls, friendship, relationships, basically everything you can imagine. Though this was a work of fiction, I have never felt closer to a character. One of the best parts o</span>
  <span id="freeText6830437141643732525" style="display:none">I honestly don't think there will ever be a book that not only captures the essence of girls, but is also the most heart-wrenching and tear inducing book I will ever read. This book starts off when Cordelia Kenn is only 15. 808 pages later she is twenty and two months old. The reader, girl or boy, learns more about life, love, girls, friendship, relationships, basically everything you can imagine. Though this was a work of fiction, I have never felt closer to a character. One of the best parts of this book is how it jumped around. Though some may not have liked that, I did. Especially towards the end of the book when everything with that creeper Cal happened. The great thing was that it kept you interested, but because the author jumped around you didn't get bored and rush through it. If you read all of the extra parts, it made the juicy parts that much more interesting. I have to say, when I first started reading I would look through to see the beginnings of the next books and I accidently read the beginning of the sixth book a long time before I got there (since I MAY have stopped reading for a VERY long time around the middle...) and I didn't think that it would affect me the way it did since I already knew. The thing is that I had that hope that maybe I read it wrong, or that hope that it'll still turn out all right. But I wasn't and so almost the entire time reading the six book, the blue box, I cried with unrestraint. I don't think that I have ever cried that much or that hard for any other book, even during the 7th Harry Potter book, or so many others, this one, because you knew Cordelia so well, so completely, so much that you felt you knew more about her then anyone else, and I think that's why when you started reading the sixth book it was like you knew exactly what Julie and Arry were going through. When I came to the end of the book, to that 808th page, my tears had finally dried up, because it was probably the most perfect ending to this book. The is a quote from this book that says "To live is to remember, to die is to forget." And I think that there are no truer words ever spoken.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer59412014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16677875840727850290">This book is truly amazing. Ipicked it up in the library and thought it would be some trashy quick (well, quick for the length of it) read about a pregnant girl; oh no, it was much more than that!<br/><br/>Cordelia is a very complex character and though this could put some people off, but drew me in. Part of the charm of the book being so long is that by the end you've read so much about her that she really seems real, you can even imagine her reactions in different situations... or I can at least. Her vo</span>
  <span id="freeText16677875840727850290" style="display:none">This book is truly amazing. Ipicked it up in the library and thought it would be some trashy quick (well, quick for the length of it) read about a pregnant girl; oh no, it was much more than that!<br /><br />Cordelia is a very complex character and though this could put some people off, but drew me in. Part of the charm of the book being so long is that by the end you've read so much about her that she really seems real, you can even imagine her reactions in different situations... or I can at least. Her voice isn't one of an 'average' teenage girl, but then again, she isn't average, she thinks deeply about things and enjoys learning; qualities which are often overlooked in characters for those who are more 'shocking,' 'outrageous' or 'original.' I found her very refreshingly different, and like several other reviewers were very suprised that the book was actually written by a 70 year old!<br /><br />I didn't find the a/b format at all annoying, as it gives a chance for the story to run alongside the other thoughts, essays and jottings that Cordelia had in her Pillow Book. Infact, the 'a' section is one of my favourite parts because it allows her to develope as a character independent of the story line.<br /><br />I love the range of themes and topics This Is All covers, brought in by characters other than Cordelia. Helpfully there is an index to look up topics, and I am so happy there is because if I'm thinking about something I really do find This Is All to be very helpful and thought provoking. Even though I first read this book about 4 years ago, I think of something from it at least every week, and often pick it up to read over my favourite bits. And the end STILL makes me cry. Buckets.<br /><br />All in all, one of the best books I have ever read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16677875840727850290" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17359915190263734205">"When I've read a book which I really like,a book which MATTERS, I feel it belongs to me." Cordelia is NOT talking about THIS IS ALL, but that's the way we all feel while and after reading this amazing book. Cordelia is organizing her journals and 'pillow books' as a gift to her unborn daughter. She wants the baby to know her mother and father as young people, how they met and fell in love, what tore them apart, and what ultimately brings them together again. Cordelia is wise, passionate, observ</span>
  <span id="freeText17359915190263734205" style="display:none">"When I've read a book which I really like,a book which MATTERS, I feel it belongs to me." Cordelia is NOT talking about THIS IS ALL, but that's the way we all feel while and after reading this amazing book. Cordelia is organizing her journals and 'pillow books' as a gift to her unborn daughter. She wants the baby to know her mother and father as young people, how they met and fell in love, what tore them apart, and what ultimately brings them together again. Cordelia is wise, passionate, observant. She brings all her gifts to everything she does. Her passion for reading is matched by her passion for writing: "I want to write so that what I write and the way I write is ME." <br /><br />Aidan Chambers has captured the voice of a young, maturing woman masterfully. I was unsure if a grown man could do it, but I think he does. All her reflections and her stories ring true. <br /><br />What doesn't ring true to me is the ending. I think a woman writer would not pull the twist at the end. I think a man's analytical mind might not be as sensitive to the emotional ramifications of his choices. <br /><br />I see why so many passionate, talented young women are drawn to Cordelia...they see themselves in her struggles and yearnings. Paige reads this every year...I can see why she does. This one will stay in my heart.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17359915190263734205" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2kT4mqe8JwUSzm6dPtZRv6dHcPV59D5o2ILNbT5+d0BzTIOJL+upNQbpPb+XIMKkRuqljSsxTXCx+CjkaW8vBQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

In a fit of preemptive grief and magical thinking, she sets out for a hike in the Galilee, leaving no forwarding information for the “notifiers” who might darken her door with the worst possible news. Recently estranged from her husband, Ilan, she drags along an unlikely companion: their former best friend and her former lover Avram, once a brilliant artistic spirit. Avram served in the army alongside Ilan when they were young, but their lives were forever changed one weekend when the two jokingly had Ora draw lots to see which of them would get the few days’ leave being offered by their commander—a chance act that sent Avram into Egpyt and the Yom Kippur War, where he was brutally tortured as POW. \n\nIn the aftermath, a virtual hermit, he refused to keep in touch with the family and has never met the boy. Now, as Ora and Avram sleep out in the hills, ford rivers, and cross valleys, avoiding all news from the front, she gives him the gift of Ofer, word by word; she supplies the whole story of her motherhood, a retelling that keeps Ofer very much alive for Ora and for the reader, and opens Avram to human bonds undreamed of in his broken world. Their walk has a “war and peace” rhythm, as their conversation places the most hideous trials of war next to the joys and anguish of raising children. Never have we seen so clearly the reality and surreality of daily life in Israel, the currents of ambivalence about war within one household, and the burdens that fall on each generation anew. \n\nGrossman’s rich imagining of a family in love and crisis makes for one of the great antiwar novels of our time.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6543832860239131838\" href=\"#\" Eventually the children landed in the home of their paternal grandparents, but their tough-love attitudes meant conflicts became commonplace. And the ghost of Jesse’s drug-addicted father haunted the halls of the house and the memories of every family member. Struggling, Jesse succumbed to a self-destructive cycle of drug and alcohol addiction and petty crime, spending more than a decade on and off the streets, often homeless. One day, he finally realized he would die unless he turned his life around.In this heartwarming and heartbreaking memoir, Jesse Thistle writes honestly and fearlessly about his painful experiences with abuse, uncovering the truth about his parents, and how he found his way back into the circle of his Indigenous culture and family through education.An eloquent exploration of what it means to       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/58103.Aidan_Chambers">About Aidan Chambers</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2579226349324808769">Born near Chester-le-Street, County Durham in 1934, Chambers was an only child, and a poor scholar; considered "slow" by his teachers, he did not learn to read fluently until the age of nine. After two years in the Royal Navy as part of his National Service, Chambers trained as a teacher and taught for three years at Westcliff High School in Southend on Sea before joining an Anglican monastery in </span>
  <span id="freeText2579226349324808769" style="display:none">Born near Chester-le-Street, County Durham in 1934, Chambers was an only child, and a poor scholar; considered "slow" by his teachers, he did not learn to read fluently until the age of nine. After two years in the Royal Navy as part of his National Service, Chambers trained as a teacher and taught for three years at Westcliff High School in Southend on Sea before joining an Anglican monastery in Stroud, Gloucestershire in 1960. He later used his experience as a monk in his novel Now I Know.<br /><br />His first plays, including <i>Johnny Salter</i> (1966), <i>The Car and The Chicken Run </i>(1968), were published while he was a teacher in Stroud.<br /><br />Chambers left the monastery in 1967 and a year later became a freelance writer. His works include the "Dance sequence" of six novels: <i>Breaktime, Dance on My Grave, Now I Know, The Toll Bridge, Postcards from No Man's Land</i> and <i>This Is All: The Pillow Book of Cordelia Kenn</i>. He and his wife, Nancy, founded Thimble Press and the magazine Signal to promote literature for children and young adults. They were awarded the Eleanor Farjeon Award for outstanding services to children's books in 1982. From 2003 to 2006 he was President of the School Library Association.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2579226349324808769" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/467879-this-is-all-the-pillow-book-of-cordelia-kenn">Quotes from This is All: The ...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Love, being in love, isn’t a constant thing. It doesn’t always flow at the same strength. It’s not always like a river in flood. It’s more like the sea. It has tides, it ebbs and flows. The thing is, when love is real, whether it’s ebbing or flowing, it’s always there, it never goes away. And that’s the only proof you can have that it is real, and not just a crush or an infatuation or a passing fancy&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/131146-love-being-in-love-isn-t-a-constant-thing-it-doesn-t">176 likes</a>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I’ve always been a slow learner in some areas of my life.mostly the areas known as myself. Or maybe I should say ‘selves.’because the fact is, I’ve never, even as a child, felt I’m only one self, only one person. I’ve always felt I’m quite a few more than one. For example, there’s my jokey self, there’s my morose and fed-up self,there’s my lewd and disgusting self. There’s my clever-clogs self, and my fading-violet-who-cant-make-up-her-mind-about-anything self. There’s my untidy-clothes-everywhere-all-over-my-room self, and my manically tidy self when I want my room to be minimalist and Zen to the nth degree. There’s my confidant, arrogant self and my polite and reasonable and good listener self. There’s my self-righteous self and my wickedly bad self, my flaky self and my bsentimental self. There are selfs I like and selfs I don’t like.there’s my little-girl selfnwhonlikes to play silly games and there’s my old-woman self when I’m quite sure I’m eighty and edging towards geriatric.<br />The self I show in action at any moment depends on where I am, who I’m with, the circumstances of the situation and the mood I’m in.&rdquo;</span>
